{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_013XaVYwwrBYUF6geEyrzPRS","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→Apache Kafka\n     2→=================\n     3→See our [web site](https://kafka.apache.org) for details on the project.\n     4→\n     5→You need to have [Java](http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javase/downloads/index.html) installed.\n     6→\n     7→We build and test Apache Kafka with Java 8, 11, 17 and 21. We set the `release` parameter in javac and scalac\n     8→to `8` to ensure the generated binaries are compatible with Java 8 or higher (independently of the Java version\n     9→used for compilation). Java 8 support project-wide has been deprecated since Apache Kafka 3.0, Java 11 support for\n    10→the broker and tools has been deprecated since Apache Kafka 3.7 and removal of both is planned for Apache Kafka 4.0 (\n    11→see [KIP-750](https://cwiki.apache.org/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=181308223) and\n    12→[KIP-1013](https://cwiki.apache.org/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=284789510) for more details).\n    13→\n    14→Scala 2.12 and 2.13 are supported and 2.13 is used by default. Scala 2.12 support has been deprecated since\n    15→Apache Kafka 3.0 and will be removed in Apache Kafka 4.0 (see [KIP-751](https://cwiki.apache.org/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=181308218)\n    16→for more details). See below for how to use a specific Scala version or all of the supported Scala versions.\n    17→\n    18→### Build a jar and run it ###\n    19→    ./gradlew jar\n    20→\n    21→Follow instructions in https://kafka.apache.org/quickstart\n    22→\n    23→### Build source jar ###\n    24→    ./gradlew srcJar\n    25→\n    26→### Build aggregated javadoc ###\n    27→    ./gradlew aggregatedJavadoc\n    28→\n    29→### Build javadoc and scaladoc ###\n    30→    ./gradlew javadoc\n    31→    ./gradlew javadocJar # builds a javadoc jar for each module\n    32→    ./gradlew scaladoc\n    33→    ./gradlew scaladocJar # builds a scaladoc jar for each module\n    34→    ./gradlew docsJar # builds both (if applicable) javadoc and scaladoc jars for each module\n    35→\n    36→### Run unit/integration tests ###\n    37→    ./gradlew test # runs both unit and integration tests\n    38→    ./gradlew unitTest\n    39→    ./gradlew integrationTest\n    40→    \n    41→### Force re-running tests without code change ###\n    42→    ./gradlew test --rerun\n    43→    ./gradlew unitTest --rerun\n    44→    ./gradlew integrationTest --rerun\n    45→\n    46→### Running a particular unit/integration test ###\n    47→    ./gradlew clients:test --tests RequestResponseTest\n    48→\n    49→### Repeatedly running a particular unit/integration test ###\n    50→    I=0; while ./gradlew clients:test --tests RequestResponseTest --rerun --fail-fast; do (( I=$I+1 )); echo \"Completed run: $I\"; sleep 1; done\n    51→\n    52→### Running a particular test method within a unit/integration test ###\n    53→    ./gradlew core:test --tests kafka.api.ProducerFailureHandlingTest.testCannotSendToInternalTopic\n    54→    ./gradlew clients:test --tests org.apache.kafka.clients.MetadataTest.testTimeToNextUpdate\n    55→\n    56→### Running a particular unit/integration test with log4j output ###\n    57→By default, there will be only small number of logs output while testing. You can adjust it by changing the `log4j.properties` file in the module's `src/test/resources` directory.\n    58→\n    59→For example, if you want to see more logs for clients project tests, you can modify [the line](https://github.com/apache/kafka/blob/trunk/clients/src/test/resources/log4j.properties#L21) in `clients/src/test/resources/log4j.properties` \n    60→to `log4j.logger.org.apache.kafka=INFO` and then run:\n    61→    \n    62→    ./gradlew cleanTest clients:test --tests NetworkClientTest   \n    63→\n    64→And you should see `INFO` level logs in the file under the `clients/build/test-results/test` directory.\n    65→\n    66→### Specifying test retries ###\n    67→By default, each failed test is retried once up to a maximum of five retries per test run. Tests are retried at the end of the test task. Adjust these parameters in the following way:\n    68→\n    69→    ./gradlew test -PmaxTestRetries=1 -PmaxTestRetryFailures=5\n    70→    \n    71→See [Test Retry Gradle Plugin](https://github.com/gradle/test-retry-gradle-plugin) for more details.\n    72→\n    73→### Generating test coverage reports ###\n    74→Generate coverage reports for the whole project:\n    75→\n    76→    ./gradlew reportCoverage -PenableTestCoverage=true -Dorg.gradle.parallel=false\n    77→\n    78→Generate coverage for a single module, i.e.: \n    79→\n    80→    ./gradlew clients:reportCoverage -PenableTestCoverage=true -Dorg.gradle.parallel=false\n    81→    \n    82→### Building a binary release gzipped tar ball ###\n    83→    ./gradlew clean releaseTarGz\n    84→\n    85→The release file can be found inside `./core/build/distributions/`.\n    86→\n    87→### Building auto generated messages ###\n    88→Sometimes it is only necessary to rebuild the RPC auto-generated message data when switching between branches, as they could\n    89→fail due to code changes. You can just run:\n    90→ \n    91→    ./gradlew processMessages processTestMessages\n    92→\n    93→### Running a Kafka broker in KRaft mode\n    94→\n    95→Using compiled files:\n    96→\n    97→    KAFKA_CLUSTER_ID=\"$(./bin/kafka-storage.sh random-uuid)\"\n    98→    ./bin/kafka-storage.sh format -t $KAFKA_CLUSTER_ID -c config/kraft/server.properties\n    99→    ./bin/kafka-server-start.sh config/kraft/server.properties\n   100→\n   101→Using docker image:\n   102→\n   103→    docker run -p 9092:9092 apache/kafka:3.7.0\n   104→\n   105→### Running a Kafka broker in ZooKeeper mode\n   106→\n   107→Using compiled files:\n   108→\n   109→    ./bin/zookeeper-server-start.sh config/zookeeper.properties\n   110→    ./bin/kafka-server-start.sh config/server.properties\n   111→\n   112→>Since ZooKeeper mode is already deprecated and planned to be removed in Apache Kafka 4.0, the docker image only supports running in KRaft mode\n   113→\n   114→### Cleaning the build ###\n   115→    ./gradlew clean\n   116→\n   117→### Running a task with one of the Scala versions available (2.12.x or 2.13.x) ###\n   118→*Note that if building the jars with a version other than 2.13.x, you need to set the `SCALA_VERSION` variable or change it in `bin/kafka-run-class.sh` to run the quick start.*\n   119→\n   120→You can pass either the major version (eg 2.12) or the full version (eg 2.12.7):\n   121→\n   122→    ./gradlew -PscalaVersion=2.12 jar\n   123→    ./gradlew -PscalaVersion=2.12 test\n   124→    ./gradlew -PscalaVersion=2.12 releaseTarGz\n   125→\n   126→### Running a task with all the scala versions enabled by default ###\n   127→\n   128→Invoke the `gradlewAll` script followed by the task(s):\n   129→\n   130→    ./gradlewAll test\n   131→    ./gradlewAll jar\n   132→    ./gradlewAll releaseTarGz\n   133→\n   134→### Running a task for a specific project ###\n   135→This is for `core`, `examples` and `clients`\n   136→\n   137→    ./gradlew core:jar\n   138→    ./gradlew core:test\n   139→\n   140→Streams has multiple sub-projects, but you can run all the tests:\n   141→\n   142→    ./gradlew :streams:testAll\n   143→\n   144→### Listing all gradle tasks ###\n   145→    ./gradlew tasks\n   146→\n   147→### Building IDE project ####\n   148→*Note that this is not strictly necessary (IntelliJ IDEA has good built-in support for Gradle projects, for example).*\n   149→\n   150→    ./gradlew eclipse\n   151→    ./gradlew idea\n   152→\n   153→The `eclipse` task has been configured to use `${project_dir}/build_eclipse` as Eclipse's build directory. Eclipse's default\n   154→build directory (`${project_dir}/bin`) clashes with Kafka's scripts directory and we don't use Gradle's build directory\n   155→to avoid known issues with this configuration.\n   156→\n   157→### Publishing the jar for all versions of Scala and for all projects to maven ###\n   158→The recommended command is:\n   159→\n   160→    ./gradlewAll publish\n   161→\n   162→For backwards compatibility, the following also works:\n   163→\n   164→    ./gradlewAll uploadArchives\n   165→\n   166→Please note for this to work you should create/update mavenPassword=\n   171→    signing.keyId=\n   172→    signing.password=\n   173→    signing.secretKeyRingFile=\n   174→\n   175→### Publishing the streams quickstart archetype artifact to maven ###\n   176→For the Streams archetype project, one cannot use gradle to upload to maven; instead the `mvn deploy` command needs to be called at the quickstart folder:\n   177→\n   178→    cd streams/quickstart\n   179→    mvn deploy\n   180→\n   181→Please note for this to work you should create/update user maven settings (typically, `${USER_HOME}/.m2/settings.xml`) to assign the following variables\n   182→\n   183→    <settings xmlns=\"http://maven.apache.org/SETTINGS/1.0.0\"\n   184→       xmlns:xsi=\"http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance\"\n   185→       xsi:schemaLocation=\"http://maven.apache.org/SETTINGS/1.0.0\n   186→                           https://maven.apache.org/xsd/settings-1.0.0.xsd\">\n   187→    ...                           \n   188→    <servers>\n   189→       ...\n   190→       <server>\n   191→          <id>apache.snapshots.https</id>\n   192→          <username>${maven_username}</username>\n   193→          <password>${maven_password}</password>\n   194→       </server>\n   195→       <server>\n   196→          <id>apache.releases.https</id>\n   197→          <username>${maven_username}</username>\n   198→          <password>${maven_password}</password>\n   199→        </server>\n   200→        ...\n   201→     </servers>\n   202→     ...\n   203→\n   204→\n   205→### Installing ALL the jars to the local Maven repository ###\n   206→The recommended command to build for both Scala 2.12 and 2.13 is:\n   207→\n   208→    ./gradlewAll publishToMavenLocal\n   209→\n   210→For backwards compatibility, the following also works:\n   211→\n   212→    ./gradlewAll install\n   213→\n   214→### Installing specific projects to the local Maven repository ###\n   215→\n   216→    ./gradlew -PskipSigning=true :streams:publishToMavenLocal\n   217→    \n   218→If needed, you can specify the Scala version with `-PscalaVersion=2.13`.\n   219→\n   220→### Building the test jar ###\n   221→    ./gradlew testJar\n   222→\n   223→### Running code quality checks ###\n   224→There are two code quality analysis tools that we regularly run, spotbugs and checkstyle.\n   225→\n   226→#### Checkstyle ####\n   227→Checkstyle enforces a consistent coding style in Kafka.\n   228→You can run checkstyle using:\n   229→\n   230→    ./gradlew checkstyleMain checkstyleTest spotlessCheck\n   231→\n   232→The checkstyle warnings will be found in `reports/checkstyle/reports/main.html` and `reports/checkstyle/reports/test.html` files in the\n   233→subproject build directories. They are also printed to the console. The build will fail if Checkstyle fails.\n   234→\n   235→**Please note that `./gradlew spotlessCheck` currently has an issue with Java 21 (see https://github.com/diffplug/spotless/pull/1920), so make sure to run this with JDK 11 or 17**\n   236→\n   237→#### Spotless ####\n   238→The import order is a part of static check. please call `spotlessApply` (require JDK 11+) to optimize the imports of Java codes before filing pull request.\n   239→\n   240→    ./gradlew spotlessApply\n   241→\n   242→**Please note that `./gradlew spotlessApply` currently has an issue with Java 21 (see https://github.com/diffplug/spotless/pull/1920), so make sure to run this with JDK 11 or 17**\n   243→\n   244→#### Spotbugs ####\n   245→Spotbugs uses static analysis to look for bugs in the code.\n   246→You can run spotbugs using:\n   247→\n   248→    ./gradlew spotbugsMain spotbugsTest -x test\n   249→\n   250→The spotbugs warnings will be found in `reports/spotbugs/main.html` and `reports/spotbugs/test.html` files in the subproject build\n   251→directories.  Use -PxmlSpotBugsReport=true to generate an XML report instead of an HTML one.\n   252→\n   253→### JMH microbenchmarks ###\n   254→We use [JMH](https://openjdk.java.net/projects/code-tools/jmh/) to write microbenchmarks that produce reliable results in the JVM.\n   255→    \n   256→See [jmh-benchmarks/README.md](https://github.com/apache/kafka/blob/trunk/jmh-benchmarks/README.md) for details on how to run the microbenchmarks.\n   257→\n   258→### Dependency Analysis ###\n   259→\n   260→The gradle [dependency debugging documentation](https://docs.gradle.org/current/userguide/viewing_debugging_dependencies.html) mentions using the `dependencies` or `dependencyInsight` tasks to debug dependencies for the root project or individual subprojects.\n   261→\n   262→Alternatively, use the `allDeps` or `allDepInsight` tasks for recursively iterating through all subprojects:\n   263→\n   264→    ./gradlew allDeps\n   265→\n   266→    ./gradlew allDepInsight --configuration runtimeClasspath --dependency com.fasterxml.jackson.core:jackson-databind\n   267→\n   268→These take the same arguments as the builtin variants.\n   269→\n   270→### Determining if any dependencies could be updated ###\n   271→    ./gradlew dependencyUpdates\n   272→\n   273→### Common build options ###\n   274→\n   275→The following options should be set with a `-P` switch, for example `./gradlew -PmaxParallelForks=1 test`.\n   276→\n   277→* `commitId`: sets the build commit ID as .git/HEAD might not be correct if there are local commits added for build purposes.\n   278→* `mavenUrl`: sets the URL of the maven deployment repository (`file://path/to/repo` can be used to point to a local repository).\n   279→* `maxParallelForks`: maximum number of test processes to start in parallel. Defaults to the number of processors available to the JVM.\n   280→* `maxScalacThreads`: maximum number of worker threads for the scalac backend. Defaults to the lowest of `8` and the number of processors\n   281→available to the JVM. The value must be between 1 and 16 (inclusive). \n   282→* `ignoreFailures`: ignore test failures from junit\n   283→* `showStandardStreams`: shows standard out and standard error of the test JVM(s) on the console.\n   284→* `skipSigning`: skips signing of artifacts.\n   285→* `testLoggingEvents`: unit test events to be logged, separated by comma. For example `./gradlew -PtestLoggingEvents=started,passed,skipped,failed test`.\n   286→* `xmlSpotBugsReport`: enable XML reports for spotBugs. This also disables HTML reports as only one can be enabled at a time.\n   287→* `maxTestRetries`: maximum number of retries for a failing test case.\n   288→* `maxTestRetryFailures`: maximum number of test failures before retrying is disabled for subsequent tests.\n   289→* `enableTestCoverage`: enables test coverage plugins and tasks, including bytecode enhancement of classes required to track said\n   290→coverage. Note that this introduces some overhead when running tests and hence why it's disabled by default (the overhead\n   291→varies, but 15-20% is a reasonable estimate).\n   292→* `keepAliveMode`: configures the keep alive mode for the Gradle compilation daemon - reuse improves start-up time. The values should \n   293→be one of `daemon` or `session` (the default is `daemon`). `daemon` keeps the daemon alive until it's explicitly stopped while\n   294→`session` keeps it alive until the end of the build session. This currently only affects the Scala compiler, see\n   295→https://github.com/gradle/gradle/pull/21034 for a PR that attempts to do the same for the Java compiler.\n   296→* `scalaOptimizerMode`: configures the optimizing behavior of the scala compiler, the value should be one of `none`, `method`, `inline-kafka` or\n   297→`inline-scala` (the default is `inline-kafka`). `none` is the scala compiler default, which only eliminates unreachable code. `method` also\n   298→includes method-local optimizations. `inline-kafka` adds inlining of methods within the kafka packages. Finally, `inline-scala` also\n   299→includes inlining of methods within the scala library (which avoids lambda allocations for methods like `Option.exists`). `inline-scala` is\n   300→only safe if the Scala library version is the same at compile time and runtime. Since we cannot guarantee this for all cases (for example, users\n   301→may depend on the kafka jar for integration tests where they may include a scala library with a different version), we don't enable it by\n   302→default. See https://www.lightbend.com/blog/scala-inliner-optimizer for more details.\n   303→\n   304→### Running system tests ###\n   305→\n   306→See [tests/README.md](tests/README.md).\n   307→\n   308→### Running in Vagrant ###\n   309→\n   310→See [vagrant/README.md](vagrant/README.md).\n   311→\n   312→### Contribution ###\n   313→\n   314→Apache Kafka is interested in building the community; we would welcome any thoughts or [patches](https://issues.apache.org/jira/browse/KAFKA). You can reach us [on the Apache mailing lists](http://kafka.apache.org/contact.html).\n   315→\n   316→To contribute follow the instructions here:\n   317→ * https://kafka.apache.org/contributing.html \n   318→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01XS8zrTuRgwuVyQBXUevUYx","session_id":"8d9d71df-a905-4759-9ef6-1a2aab12fc4d","uuid":"5e260966-0d8d-4efd-83da-a7cf798d0c03"}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_0171Nf9TJ3YzRy9xznCf2UuD","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→Trogdor\n     2→========================================\n     3→Trogdor is a test framework for Apache Kafka.\n     4→\n     5→Trogdor can run benchmarks and other workloads.  Trogdor can also inject faults in order to stress test the system.\n     6→\n     7→Quickstart\n     8→=========================================================\n     9→First, we want to start a single-node Kafka cluster with a ZooKeeper and a broker.\n    10→\n    11→Running ZooKeeper:\n    12→\n    13→```\n    14→ ./bin/zookeeper-server-start.sh ./config/zookeeper.properties &> /tmp/zookeeper.log &\n    15→```\n    16→Running Kafka:\n    17→\n    18→```\n    19→./bin/kafka-server-start.sh ./config/server.properties &> /tmp/kafka.log &\n    20→```\n    21→Then, we want to run a Trogdor Agent, plus a Trogdor Coordinator.\n    22→\n    23→To run the Trogdor Agent:\n    24→\n    25→```\n    26→./bin/trogdor.sh agent -c ./config/trogdor.conf -n node0 &> /tmp/trogdor-agent.log &\n    27→```\n    28→To run the Trogdor Coordinator:\n    29→\n    30→```\n    31→./bin/trogdor.sh coordinator -c ./config/trogdor.conf -n node0 &> /tmp/trogdor-coordinator.log &\n    32→```\n    33→Let's confirm that all of the daemons are running:\n    34→\n    35→```\n    36→jps\n    37→116212 Coordinator\n    38→115188 QuorumPeerMain\n    39→116571 Jps\n    40→115420 Kafka\n    41→115694 Agent\n    42→```\n    43→Now, we can submit a test job to Trogdor.\n    44→\n    45→```\n    46→./bin/trogdor.sh client createTask -t localhost:8889 -i produce0 --spec ./tests/spec/simple_produce_bench.json\n    47→Sent CreateTaskRequest for task produce0.\n    48→```\n    49→We can run showTask to see what the task's status is:\n    50→\n    51→```\n    52→./bin/trogdor.sh client showTask -t localhost:8889 -i produce0\n    53→Task bar of type org.apache.kafka.trogdor.workload.ProduceBenchSpec is DONE. FINISHED at 2019-01-09T20:38:22.039-08:00 after 6s\n    54→```\n    55→To see the results, we use showTask with --show-status:\n    56→\n    57→```\n    58→./bin/trogdor.sh client showTask -t localhost:8889 -i produce0 --show-status\n    59→Task bar of type org.apache.kafka.trogdor.workload.ProduceBenchSpec is DONE. FINISHED at 2019-01-09T20:38:22.039-08:00 after 6s\n    60→Status: {\n    61→  \"totalSent\" : 50000,\n    62→  \"averageLatencyMs\" : 17.83388,\n    63→  \"p50LatencyMs\" : 12,\n    64→  \"p95LatencyMs\" : 75,\n    65→  \"p99LatencyMs\" : 96,\n    66→  \"transactionsCommitted\" : 0\n    67→}\n    68→```\n    69→Trogdor Architecture\n    70→========================================\n    71→Trogdor has a single coordinator process which manages multiple agent processes.  Each agent process is responsible for a single cluster node.\n    72→\n    73→The Trogdor coordinator manages tasks.  A task is anything we might want to do on a cluster, such as running a benchmark, injecting a fault, or running a workload.  In order to implement each task, the coordinator creates workers on one or more agent nodes.\n    74→\n    75→The Trogdor agent process implements the tasks.  For example, when running a workload, the agent process is the process which produces and consumes messages.\n    76→\n    77→Both the coordinator and the agent expose a REST interface that accepts objects serialized via JSON.  There is also a command-line program which makes it easy to send messages to either one without manually crafting the JSON message body.\n    78→\n    79→All Trogdor RPCs are idempotent except the shutdown requests.  Sending an idempotent RPC twice in a row has the same effect as sending the RPC once.\n    80→\n    81→Tasks\n    82→========================================\n    83→Tasks are described by specifications containing:\n    84→\n    85→* A \"class\" field describing the task type.  This contains a full Java class name.\n    86→* A \"startMs\" field describing when the task should start.  This is given in terms of milliseconds since the UNIX epoch.\n    87→* A \"durationMs\" field describing how long the task should last.  This is given in terms of milliseconds.\n    88→* Other fields which are task-specific.\n    89→\n    90→The task specification is usually written as JSON.  For example, this task specification describes a network partition between nodes 1 and 2, and 3:\n    91→\n    92→    {\n    93→        \"class\": \"org.apache.kafka.trogdor.fault.NetworkPartitionFaultSpec\",\n    94→        \"startMs\": 1000,\n    95→        \"durationMs\": 30000,\n    96→        \"partitions\": [[\"node1\", \"node2\"], [\"node3\"]]\n    97→    }\n    98→    \n    99→This task runs a simple ProduceBench test on a cluster with one producer node, 5 topics, and 10,000 messages per second. \n   100→The keys are generated sequentially and the configured partitioner (DefaultPartitioner) is used. \n   101→\n   102→    {\n   103→        \"class\": \"org.apache.kafka.trogdor.workload.ProduceBenchSpec\",\n   104→        \"durationMs\": 10000000,\n   105→        \"producerNode\": \"node0\",\n   106→        \"bootstrapServers\": \"localhost:9092\",\n   107→        \"targetMessagesPerSec\": 10000,\n   108→        \"maxMessages\": 50000,\n   109→        \"activeTopics\": {\n   110→            \"foo[1-3]\": {\n   111→                \"numPartitions\": 10,\n   112→                \"replicationFactor\": 1\n   113→            }\n   114→        },\n   115→        \"inactiveTopics\": {\n   116→             \"foo[4-5]\": {\n   117→                 \"numPartitions\": 10,\n   118→                 \"replicationFactor\": 1\n   119→             }\n   120→        },\n   121→        \"keyGenerator\": {\n   122→             \"type\": \"sequential\",\n   123→             \"size\": 8,\n   124→             \"offset\": 1\n   125→        },\n   126→        \"useConfiguredPartitioner\": true\n   127→     }\n   128→\n   129→Tasks are submitted to the coordinator.  Once the coordinator determines that it is time for the task to start, it creates workers on agent processes.  The workers run until the task is done.\n   130→\n   131→Task specifications are immutable; they do not change after the task has been created.\n   132→\n   133→Tasks can be in several states:\n   134→* PENDING, when task is waiting to execute,\n   135→* RUNNING, when the task is running,\n   136→* STOPPING, when the task is in the process of stopping,\n   137→* DONE, when the task is done.\n   138→\n   139→Tasks that are DONE also have an error field which will be set if the task failed.\n   140→\n   141→Workloads\n   142→========================================\n   143→Trogdor can run several workloads.  Workloads perform operations on the cluster and measure their performance.  Workloads fail when the operations cannot be performed.\n   144→\n   145→### ProduceBench\n   146→ProduceBench starts a Kafka producer on a single agent node, producing to several partitions.  The workload measures the average produce latency, as well as the median, 95th percentile, and 99th percentile latency.\n   147→It can be configured to use a transactional producer which can commit transactions based on a set time interval or number of messages.\n   148→\n   149→### RoundTripWorkload\n   150→RoundTripWorkload tests both production and consumption.  The workload starts a Kafka producer and consumer on a single node.  The consumer will read back the messages that were produced by the producer.\n   151→\n   152→### ConsumeBench\n   153→ConsumeBench starts one or more Kafka consumers on a single agent node. Depending on the passed in configuration (see ConsumeBenchSpec), the consumers either subscribe to a set of topics (leveraging consumer group functionality and dynamic partition assignment) or manually assign partitions to themselves.\n   154→The workload measures the average consume latency, as well as the median, 95th percentile, and 99th percentile latency.\n   155→\n   156→Faults\n   157→========================================\n   158→Trogdor can run several faults which deliberately break something in the cluster.\n   159→\n   160→### ProcessStopFault\n   161→ProcessStopFault stops a process by sending it a SIGSTOP signal.  When the fault ends, the process is resumed with SIGCONT.\n   162→\n   163→### NetworkPartitionFault\n   164→NetworkPartitionFault sets up an artificial network partition between one or more sets of nodes.  Currently, this is implemented using iptables.  The iptables rules are set up on the outbound traffic from the affected nodes.  Therefore, the affected nodes should still be reachable from outside the cluster.\n   165→\n   166→External Processes\n   167→========================================\n   168→Trogdor supports running arbitrary commands in external processes. This is a generic way to run any configurable command in the Trogdor framework - be it a Python program, bash script, docker image, etc.\n   169→\n   170→### ExternalCommandWorker\n   171→ExternalCommandWorker starts an external command defined by the ExternalCommandSpec. It essentially allows you to run any command on any Trogdor agent node.\n   172→The worker communicates with the external process via its stdin, stdout and stderr in a JSON protocol. It uses stdout for any actionable communication and only logs what it sees in stderr.\n   173→On startup the worker will first send a message describing the workload to the external process in this format:\n   174→```\n   175→{\"id\":<task ID string>, \"workload\":<configured workload JSON object>}\n   176→```\n   177→and will then listen for messages from the external process, again in a JSON format.\n   178→Said JSON can contain the following fields:\n   179→- status: If the object contains this field, the status of the worker will be set to the given value.\n   180→- error: If the object contains this field, the error of the worker will be set to the given value. Once an error occurs, the external process will be terminated.\n   181→- log: If the object contains this field, a log message will be issued with this text.\n   182→An example:\n   183→```json\n   184→{\"log\": \"Finished successfully.\", \"status\": {\"p99ProduceLatency\": \"100ms\", \"messagesSent\": 10000}}\n   185→```\n   186→\n   187→Exec Mode\n   188→========================================\n   189→Sometimes, you just want to run a test quickly on a single node.  In this case, you can use \"exec mode.\"  This mode allows you to run a single Trogdor Agent without a Coordinator.\n   190→\n   191→When using exec mode, you must pass in a Task specification to use.  The Agent will try to start this task.\n   192→\n   193→For example:\n   194→\n   195→```\n   196→./bin/trogdor.sh agent -n node0 -c ./config/trogdor.conf --exec ./tests/spec/simple_produce_bench.json\n   197→```\n   198→When using exec mode, the Agent will exit once the task is complete.\n   199→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01XS8zrTuRgwuVyQBXUevUYx","session_id":"8d9d71df-a905-4759-9ef6-1a2aab12fc4d","uuid":"a3ac2516-eba6-4b91-aba9-8a144988cadf"}

JMH is a framework for running and analyzing benchmarks (micro or macro) written in Java (or\n     6→another JVM language).\n     7→\n     8→### Running benchmarks\n     9→\n    10→If you want to set specific JMH flags or only run certain benchmarks, passing arguments via\n    11→gradle tasks is cumbersome. These are simplified by the provided `jmh.sh` script.\n    12→\n    13→The default behavior is to run all benchmarks:\n    14→\n    15→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh\n    16→    \n    17→Pass a pattern or name after the command to select the benchmarks:\n    18→\n    19→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh LRUCacheBenchmark\n    20→\n    21→Check which benchmarks that match the provided pattern:\n    22→\n    23→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -l LRUCacheBenchmark\n    24→\n    25→Run a specific test and override the number of forks, iterations and warm-up iteration to `2`:\n    26→\n    27→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -f 2 -i 2 -wi 2 LRUCacheBenchmark\n    28→\n    29→Run a specific test with async and GC profilers on Linux and flame graph output:\n    30→\n    31→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -prof gc -prof async:libPath=/path/to/libasyncProfiler.so\\;output=flamegraph LRUCacheBenchmark\n    32→\n    33→The following sections cover async profiler and GC profilers in more detail.\n    34→\n    35→### Using JMH with async profiler\n    36→\n    37→It's good practice to check profiler output for microbenchmarks in order to verify that they represent the expected\n    38→application behavior and measure what you expect to measure. Some example pitfalls include the use of expensive mocks\n    39→or accidental inclusion of test setup code in the benchmarked code. JMH includes\n    40→[async-profiler](https://github.com/jvm-profiling-tools/async-profiler) integration that makes this easy:\n    41→\n    42→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -prof async:libPath=/path/to/libasyncProfiler.so\n    43→\n    44→With flame graph output (the semicolon is escaped to ensure it is not treated as a command separator):\n    45→\n    46→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -prof async:libPath=/path/to/libasyncProfiler.so\\;output=flamegraph\n    47→\n    48→Simultaneous cpu, allocation and lock profiling with async profiler 2.0 and jfr output (the semicolon is\n    49→escaped to ensure it is not treated as a command separator):\n    50→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. Let me compile a structured summary based on my exploration:\n\n## Apache Kafka Module/Directory Structure Overview\n\nBased on my analysis of the Apache Kafka 3.9.0 codebase, here's a comprehensive breakdown of the core modules and their purposes:\n\n### Top-Level Module Organization\n\nThe Kafka project is organized as a Gradle multi-module build (defined in `/workspace/settings.gradle`). There are **15+ major modules** plus numerous sub-modules:\n\n---\n\n## CORE MODULES\n\n### **1. Core Module** (`/workspace/core/`)\n**Purpose:** Legacy server implementation and fundamental infrastructure\n- **Location:** `/workspace/core/src/main/`\n  - **Scala:** `kafka.admin`, `kafka.api`, `kafka.cluster`, `kafka.controller`, `kafka.coordinator`, `kafka.log`, `kafka.metrics`, `kafka.migration`, `kafka.network`, `kafka.raft`, `kafka.security`, `kafka.server`, `kafka.zk`, `kafka.zookeeper`\n  - **Java:** `kafka.log`, `kafka.server.handlers`, `kafka.log.remote`, `kafka.server.share`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Broker server logic (legacy Scala implementation)\n- Log storage and management\n- Controller logic for ZooKeeper mode\n- Admin operations and cluster management\n- Legacy networking and RPC handling\n- ZooKeeper integration (being phased out in favor of KRaft)\n\n---\n\n### **2. Server Module** (`/workspace/server/`)\n**Purpose:** New modular broker implementation (KRaft-based, Java)\n- **Location:** `/workspace/server/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.network`, `org.apache.kafka.security`, `org.apache.kafka.server`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Network layer implementation\n- Security/authorization\n- Server configuration and startup logic\n- Broker request processing\n\n---\n\n### **3. Server-Common Module** (`/workspace/server-common/`)\n**Purpose:** Shared server utilities and configuration\n- **Location:** `/workspace/server-common/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.admin`, `org.apache.kafka.deferred`, `org.apache.kafka.metadata`, `org.apache.kafka.queue`, `org.apache.kafka.security`, `org.apache.kafka.server.common`, `org.apache.kafka.server.config`, `org.apache.kafka.server.fault`, `org.apache.kafka.server.group`, `org.apache.kafka.server.immutable`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Admin utilities\n- Deferred computation framework\n- Metadata management utilities\n- Queue/event processing\n- Server fault handling\n- Shared configuration management\n- Group coordination utilities (for consumer and transaction groups)\n\n---\n\n## STORAGE MODULES\n\n### **4. Storage Module** (`/workspace/storage/`)\n**Purpose:** Log segment storage and retrieval implementation\n- **Location:** `/workspace/storage/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.server.log`, `org.apache.kafka.storage.internals.checkpoint`, `org.apache.kafka.storage.internals.epoch`, `org.apache.kafka.storage.internals.log`, `org.apache.kafka.storage.log.metrics`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Tiered storage implementation\n- Log segment management\n- Remote log metadata storage\n- Checkpoint management\n- Storage metrics\n\n### **5. Metadata Module** (`/workspace/metadata/`)\n**Purpose:** KRaft metadata management and controller\n- **Location:** `/workspace/metadata/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.controller`, `org.apache.kafka.image`, `org.apache.kafka.image.loader`, `org.apache.kafka.metadata`, `org.apache.kafka.metadata.migration`, `org.apache.kafka.metadata.bootstrap`, `org.apache.kafka.metadata.placement`, `org.apache.kafka.metadata.storage`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- KRaft controller implementation\n- Metadata state machine and snapshots\n- Metadata image (in-memory cluster state)\n- Migration from ZooKeeper to KRaft\n- Bootstrap coordination\n- Replica placement logic\n- Metadata storage persistence\n\n### **7. Raft Module** (`/workspace/raft/`)\n**Purpose:** Raft consensus protocol implementation\n- **Location:** `/workspace/raft/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.raft`, `org.apache.kafka.snapshot`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Raft consensus implementation (used for KRaft mode)\n- Leader election\n- Log replication\n- Snapshot management\n- State machine interface\n\n---\n\n## COORDINATION MODULES\n\n### **8. Group-Coordinator Module** (`/workspace/group-coordinator/`)\n**Purpose:** Consumer group coordination and management\n- **Location:** `/workspace/group-coordinator/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.coordinator.group`, `org.apache.kafka.coordinator.group.assignor`, `org.apache.kafka.coordinator.group.classic`, `org.apache.kafka.coordinator.group.modern`, `org.apache.kafka.coordinator.group.metrics`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Consumer group membership management\n- Rebalancing logic\n- Partition assignment (classic and modern protocols)\n- Group metrics and monitoring\n- Share group coordination (Share API)\n\n### **9. Transaction-Coordinator Module** (`/workspace/transaction-coordinator/`)\n**Purpose:** Transaction coordination and management\n- **Location:** `/workspace/transaction-coordinator/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.coordinator.transaction`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Transactional producer coordination\n- Transaction state management\n- Exactly-once semantics enforcement\n\n---\n\n## CLIENT MODULES\n\n### **10. Clients Module** (`/workspace/clients/`)\n**Purpose:** Producer, Consumer, and Admin client libraries\n- **Location:** `/workspace/clients/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:**\n    - `org.apache.kafka.clients.admin` - Admin client\n    - `org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer` - Consumer client\n    - `org.apache.kafka.clients.producer` - Producer client\n    - `org.apache.kafka.common` - Shared client utilities, configurations, errors, metrics\n    - `org.apache.kafka.clients` - Low-level networking and coordination\n\n**Key Components:**\n- KafkaProducer and internals\n- KafkaConsumer and internals\n- Admin client for cluster management\n- Network protocol handling\n- Metadata management\n- Client-side metrics\n- Configuration management\n\n---\n\n## STREAMS MODULES\n\n### **11. Streams Module** (`/workspace/streams/`)\n**Purpose:** Stream processing library and topology\n- **Location:** `/workspace/streams/src/main/`\n  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.streams`, `org.apache.kafka.streams.kstream`, `org.apache.kafka.streams.processor`, `org.apache.kafka.streams.state`, `org.apache.kafka.streams.query`, `org.apache.kafka.streams.errors`\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Kafka Streams DSL (KStream, KTable, GlobalKTable)\n- Stream processors and topology\n- State stores and management\n- Interactive queries\n- Exactly-once/at-least-once semantics\n- Windowing operations\n- Joins (stream-stream, stream-table, foreign key)\n\n### **12. Streams Sub-modules:**\n- `streams:examples` - Example applications\n- `streams:streams-scala` - Scala DSL\n- `streams:test-utils` - Test utilities for Streams apps\n- `streams:upgrade-system-tests-*` - Backward compatibility tests\n\n---\n\n## CONNECT MODULES\n\n### **13. Connect Modules** (`/workspace/connect/`)\n**Purpose:** Kafka Connect framework for data integration\n- **Submodules:**\n  - `connect:api` - Public Connect API\n  - `connect:runtime` - Connect worker runtime\n  - `connect:file` - File source/sink connectors\n  - `connect:json` - JSON converter\n  - `connect:mirror` - MirrorMaker 2 (cluster mirroring)\n  - `connect:mirror-client` - MirrorMaker client utilities\n  - `connect:basic-auth-extension` - Authentication extension\n  - `connect:test-plugins` - Test utilities\n  - `connect:transforms` - Built-in transformations\n\n**Key Components:**\n- Source/Sink connector framework\n- Task and worker management\n- Converters and serialization\n- Cluster replication (MirrorMaker 2)\n- Schema management\n\n---\n\n## TOOLS & UTILITIES\n\n### **14. Tools Module** (`/workspace/tools/`)\n**Purpose:** Admin and management CLI tools\n- Command-line utilities for cluster management\n- Sub-module: `tools:tools-api` - API for tools\n\n### **15. Shell Module** (`/workspace/shell/`)\n**Purpose:** Interactive Kafka shell for management\n\n### **16. Generator Module** (`/workspace/generator/`)\n**Purpose:** Code generation for protocol messages\n\n### **17. Log4j-Appender Module** (`/workspace/log4j-appender/`)\n**Purpose:** Log4j integration for Kafka logging\n\n---\n\n## BENCHMARKING & TESTING\n\n### **18.
